<p>The <b>peregrine falcon</b> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>), also known as the <b>peregrine</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Heinzel1995_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinzel1995-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and historically as the <b>duck hawk</b> in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Friedmann1950_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedmann1950-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> is a widespread <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptor</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Falconidae" title="Falconidae">Falconidae</a>. A large, <a href="/wiki/Corvus_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvus (genus)">crow</a>-sized <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a>, it has a blue-grey <a href="/wiki/Human_back" title="Human back">back</a>, barred white underparts, and a black head. The peregrine is renowned for its speed, reaching over 320&#160;km/h (200&#160;mph) during its characteristic hunting stoop (high-speed dive),<sup id="cite_ref-USFWS_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> making it the fastest bird in the world, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Fastest_animals" title="Fastest animals">fastest member of the animal kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> According to a <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(U.S._TV_channel)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic (U.S. TV channel)">National Geographic</a></i> TV program, the highest measured speed of a peregrine falcon is 389&#160;km/h (242&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-Harpole2005_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harpole2005-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TerminalVelocity_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TerminalVelocity-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> As is typical for <a href="/wiki/Avivore" title="Avivore">bird-eating</a> raptors, peregrine falcons are <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a>, with females being considerably larger than males.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Peregrine falcons have a <a href="/wiki/Flicker_fusion_threshold" title="Flicker fusion threshold">flicker fusion frequency</a> of 129 Hz (cycles per second), very fast for a bird of its size, and much faster than mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine's breeding range includes land regions from the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tundra" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic tundra">Arctic tundra</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>. It can be found nearly everywhere on Earth, except extreme <a href="/wiki/Polar_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar region">polar regions</a>, very high mountains, and most <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>; the only major ice-free landmass from which it is entirely absent is <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. This makes it the world's most widespread raptor,<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and one of the most widely found bird species. In fact, the only land-based bird species found over a larger geographic area is not always naturally occurring, but one widely introduced by humans, the <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeon</a>, which in turn now supports many peregrine populations as a prey species. The peregrine is a highly successful example of <a href="/wiki/Urban_wildlife" title="Urban wildlife">urban wildlife</a> in much of its range, taking advantage of tall buildings as nest sites and an abundance of prey such as pigeons and ducks. Both the English and <a href="/wiki/Scientific_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific name">scientific names</a> of this <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> mean "wandering falcon," referring to the <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> habits of many northern populations. Experts recognize 17 to 19 <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>, which vary in appearance and range; disagreement exists over whether the distinctive <a href="/wiki/Barbary_falcon" title="Barbary falcon">Barbary falcon</a> is represented by two subspecies of <i>Falco peregrinus,</i> or is a separate species, <i>F. pelegrinoides</i>. The two species' divergence is relatively recent, during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Last glacial period">last ice age</a>, therefore the genetic differential between them (and also the difference in their appearance) is relatively tiny. They are only about 0.6–0.8% genetically differentiated.<sup id="cite_ref-Nittinger2005_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nittinger2005-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although its diet consists almost exclusively of medium-sized birds, the peregrine will occasionally hunt small mammals, small reptiles, or even insects. Reaching sexual maturity at one year, it <a href="/wiki/Mates_for_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Mates for life">mates for life</a> and nests in a <a href="/wiki/Scrape_nest" class="mw-redirect" title="Scrape nest">scrape</a>, normally on cliff edges or, in recent times, on tall human-made structures.<sup id="cite_ref-Cade1996_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cade1996-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine falcon became an endangered species in many areas because of the widespread use of certain pesticides, especially <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>. Since the ban on DDT from the early 1970s, populations have recovered, supported by large-scale protection of nesting places and releases to the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-Cade1988_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cade1988-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The peregrine falcon is a well-respected <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> bird due to its strong hunting ability, high trainability, versatility, and availability via <a href="/wiki/Captive_breeding" title="Captive breeding">captive breeding</a>. It is effective on most <a href="/wiki/Game_bird" class="mw-redirect" title="Game bird">game bird</a> species, from small to large.
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Description"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Description</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_(Australia).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Peregrine_falcon_%28Australia%29.JPG/220px-Peregrine_falcon_%28Australia%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Peregrine_falcon_%28Australia%29.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="319" data-file-height="266" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_(Australia).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Falco peregrinus</i>. <a href="/wiki/Royal_National_Park" title="Royal National Park">Royal National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, Australia</div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon has a body length of 34&#160;to 58&#160;cm (13–23&#160;in) and a wingspan from 74&#160;to 120&#160;cm (29–47&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Potter2002_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potter2002-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The male and female have similar markings and <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a>, but as in many <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> the peregrine falcon displays marked <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> in size, with the female measuring up to 30% larger than the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Scholz_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scholz-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Males weigh 330&#160;to 1,000&#160;g (0.73–2.20&#160;lb) and the noticeably larger females weigh 700&#160;to 1,500&#160;g (1.5–3.3&#160;lb). In most subspecies, males weigh less than 700&#160;g (1.5&#160;lb) and females weigh more than 800&#160;g (1.8&#160;lb), with cases of females weighing about 50% more than their male breeding mates not uncommon.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whiteetal2002_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whiteetal2002-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The standard linear measurements of peregrines are: the wing chord measures 26.5&#160;to 39&#160;cm (10.4–15.4&#160;in), the tail measures 13&#160;to 19&#160;cm (5.1–7.5&#160;in) and the tarsus measures 4.5&#160;to 5.6&#160;cm (1.8–2.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The back and the long pointed wings of the adult are usually bluish black to slate grey with indistinct darker barring (see "Subspecies" <a href="#species">below</a>); the wingtips are black.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter2002_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potter2002-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The white to rusty underparts are barred with thin clean bands of dark brown or black.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The tail, coloured like the back but with thin clean bars, is long, narrow, and rounded at the end with a black tip and a white band at the very end. The top of the head and a "moustache" along the cheeks are black, contrasting sharply with the pale sides of the neck and white throat.<sup id="cite_ref-Terres1991_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres1991-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cere" class="mw-redirect" title="Cere">cere</a> is yellow, as are the feet, and the <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">claws</a> are black.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckstead2001_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckstead2001-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The upper beak is notched near the tip, an <a href="/wiki/Adaptation_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adaptation (biology)">adaptation</a> which enables falcons to kill prey by severing the <a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebrae" title="Cervical vertebrae">spinal column</a> at the neck.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USFWS_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The immature bird is much browner with streaked, rather than barred, underparts, and has a pale bluish cere and orbital ring.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_systematics">Taxonomy and systematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and systematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Audubon-peregrinus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Audubon-peregrinus.jpg/220px-Audubon-peregrinus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Audubon-peregrinus.jpg/330px-Audubon-peregrinus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Audubon-peregrinus.jpg/440px-Audubon-peregrinus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1930" data-file-height="1184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Audubon-peregrinus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by <a href="/wiki/John_James_Audubon" title="John James Audubon">John James Audubon</a></div></div></div>
<p><i>Falco peregrinus</i> was first described under its current <a href="/wiki/Binomial_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Binomial name">binomial name</a> by English ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Marmaduke_Tunstall" title="Marmaduke Tunstall">Marmaduke Tunstall</a> in his 1771 work <i>Ornithologia Britannica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tunstall1771_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tunstall1771-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The scientific name <i>Falco peregrinus</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> phrase that was used by <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> in 1225. The specific name is taken from the fact that juvenile birds were taken while journeying to their breeding location rather than from the nest, as falcon nests were difficult to get at.<sup id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Latin term for falcon, <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">falco</i>, is related to <i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/falx#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:falx">falx</a></i>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickle</a>", in reference to the silhouette of the falcon's long, pointed wings in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-USFWS_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine falcon belongs to a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> whose lineage includes the <a href="/wiki/Hierofalcon" title="Hierofalcon">hierofalcons</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Prairie_falcon" title="Prairie falcon">prairie falcon</a> (<i>F.&#160;mexicanus</i>). This lineage probably diverged from other falcons towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">Late Miocene</a> or in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Pliocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Pliocene">Early Pliocene</a>, about 5–8&#160;<a href="/wiki/Million_years_ago" class="mw-redirect" title="Million years ago">million years ago</a> (mya). As the peregrine-hierofalcon group includes both <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and North American species, it is likely that the lineage originated in western <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> or Africa. Its relationship to other falcons is not clear, as the issue is complicated by widespread <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> confounding <a href="/wiki/MtDNA" class="mw-redirect" title="MtDNA">mtDNA</a> <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA sequence">sequence</a> analyses. For example, a genetic lineage of the <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a> (<i>F.&#160;cherrug</i>) is known<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig1994_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig1994-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink1998_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink1998-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> which originated from a male saker producing fertile young with a female peregrine ancestor, and the descendants further breeding with sakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Seibold1993_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seibold1993-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, peregrines are regularly paired in captivity with other species such as the <a href="/wiki/Lanner_falcon" title="Lanner falcon">lanner falcon</a> (<i>F.&#160;biarmicus</i>) to produce the "<a href="/wiki/Perilanner" title="Perilanner">perilanner</a>", a somewhat popular bird in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> as it combines the peregrine's hunting skill with the lanner's hardiness, or the <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcon</a> to produce large, strikingly coloured birds for the use of falconers. As can be seen, the peregrine is still genetically close to the hierofalcons, though their lineages diverged in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pliocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Pliocene">Late Pliocene</a> (maybe some 2.5–2&#160;mya in the <a href="/wiki/Gelasian" title="Gelasian">Gelasian</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Nittinger2005_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nittinger2005-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helbig1994_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig1994-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink1998_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink1998-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffiths1999_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths1999-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink2000_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2000-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Groombridge2002_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Groombridge2002-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffiths2004_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths2004-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <i>Falco peregrinus</i> have been described, with 19 accepted by the 1994 <i><a href="/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World" title="Handbook of the Birds of the World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie1961_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie1961-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> which considers the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_falcon" title="Barbary falcon">Barbary falcon</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and coastal North <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> to be two subspecies (<i>pelegrinoides</i> and <i>babylonicus</i>) of <i>Falco peregrinus,</i> rather than a distinct species, <i>F. pelegrinoides.</i> The following map shows the general ranges of these 19 subspecies
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png" class="image"><img alt="A map of the world, green shows on several continents, but there are also several big bare spots marked with E for extinct." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png/700px-PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png" decoding="async" width="700" height="390" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png/1050px-PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png/1400px-PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineSubspeciesMap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Breeding ranges of the subspecies</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg/220px-FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="317" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg/330px-FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg/440px-FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3672" data-file-height="5288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FalcoPeregrinusBabylonicusGould.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of <i>F. p. babylonicus</i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Gould" title="John Gould">John Gould</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG/220px-Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG/330px-Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG/440px-Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG 2x" data-file-width="798" data-file-height="599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_ernesti.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile of subspecies <i>ernesti</i> in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Mahawu" title="Mount Mahawu">Mount Mahawu</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sulawesi" title="North Sulawesi">North Sulawesi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg/220px-Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg/330px-Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg/440px-Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3498" data-file-height="2898" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_nest_USFWS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Adult of subspecies <i>pealei</i> or <i>tundrius</i> by its nest in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><i><b><span id="anatum"></span><span id="Falco_peregrinus_anatum">Falco peregrinus anatum</span></b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucien_Bonaparte" title="Charles Lucien Bonaparte">Bonaparte</a> in 1838,<sup id="cite_ref-AOUp164_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOUp164-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> is known as the American peregrine falcon, or "duck hawk"; its scientific name means "duck peregrine falcon". At one time, it was partly included in <i>leucogenys</i>. It is mainly found in the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> today. It was formerly common throughout North America between the tundra and northern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, where current <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> efforts seek to restore the population.<sup id="cite_ref-AOUp164_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOUp164-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Most mature <i>anatum</i>, except those that breed in more northern areas, winter in their breeding range. Most <a href="/wiki/Vagrant_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Vagrant birds">vagrants</a> that reach western Europe seem to belong to the more northern and strongly migratory <i>tundrius</i>, only considered distinct since 1968. It is similar to <i>peregrinus</i> but is slightly smaller; adults are somewhat paler and less patterned below, but juveniles are darker and more patterned below. Males weigh 500&#160;to 700&#160;g (1.1–1.5&#160;lb), while females weigh 800&#160;to 1,100&#160;g (1.8–2.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Whiteetal2002_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whiteetal2002-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It has become extinct in eastern North America, and populations there are hybrids as a result of reintroductions of birds from elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Lehr2000_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lehr2000-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><i><b>Falco peregrinus babylonicus</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sclater" title="Philip Sclater">P.L. Sclater</a> in 1861, is found in eastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_Altai" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongolian Altai">Mongolian Altai</a> ranges. A few birds winter in northern and northwestern India, mainly in dry semi-desert habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Pande2007_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande2007-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It is paler than <i><a href="/wiki/Falco_peregrinus_pelegrinoides" class="mw-redirect" title="Falco peregrinus pelegrinoides">pelegrinoides</a></i>, and somewhat similar to a small, pale <a href="/wiki/Lanner_falcon" title="Lanner falcon">lanner falcon</a> (<i>Falco biarmicus</i>). Males weigh 330 to 400 grams (12 to 14&#160;oz), while females weigh 513 to 765 grams (18.1 to 27.0&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Falco_peregrinus_brookei"></span>
</p>
<ul><li><i><b>Falco peregrinus brookei</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bowdler_Sharpe" title="Richard Bowdler Sharpe">Sharpe</a> in 1873, is also known as the Mediterranean peregrine falcon or the Maltese falcon.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> It includes <i>caucasicus</i> and most specimens of the proposed race <i>punicus</i>, though others may be <i><a href="/wiki/Falco_peregrinus_pelegrinoides" class="mw-redirect" title="Falco peregrinus pelegrinoides">pelegrinoides</a></i> (Barbary falcons), or perhaps the rare hybrids between these two which might occur around <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. They occur from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> around the Mediterranean, except in <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> regions, to the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>. They are non-migratory. It is smaller than the <a href="#nominate">nominate subspecies</a>, and the underside usually has rusty hue.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Males weigh around 445&#160;g (0.981&#160;lb), while females weigh up to 920&#160;g (2.03&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b><span id="calidus"></span><span id="Falco_peregrinus_calidus">Falco peregrinus calidus</span></b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/John_Latham_(ornithologist)" title="John Latham (ornithologist)">John Latham</a> in 1790, was formerly called <i>leucogenys</i> and includes <i>caeruleiceps</i>. It breeds in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> tundra of Eurasia, from <a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk Oblast</a> to roughly <a href="/wiki/Yana_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yana River">Yana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigirka_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Indigirka River">Indigirka Rivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>. It is completely migratory, and travels south in winter as far as <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>. It is often seen around wetland habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen2005_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen2005-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> It is paler than <i>peregrinus</i>, especially on the crown. Males weigh 588&#160;to 740&#160;g (1.296–1.631&#160;lb), while females weigh 925&#160;to 1,333&#160;g (2.039–2.939&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus cassini</b></i>, described by Sharpe in 1873, is also known as the Austral peregrine falcon. It includes <i>kreyenborgi</i>, the pallid falcon,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Leucistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Leucistic">leucistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Morph_(zoology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Morph (zoology)">morph</a> occurring in southernmost South America, which was long believed to be a distinct species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis1983_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis1983-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Its range includes South America from <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> through <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">Tierra del Fuego</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It is non-migratory. It is similar to <a href="#nominate">nominate</a>, but slightly smaller with a black ear region. The variation <i>kreyenborgi</i> is medium grey above, has little barring below, and has a head pattern like the <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a>, but the ear region is white.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis1983_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis1983-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus ernesti</b></i>, described by Sharpe in 1894, is found from the <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Islands" title="Sunda Islands">Sunda Islands</a> to <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and south to eastern <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a>. Its geographical separation from <i>nesiotes</i> requires confirmation. It is non-migratory. It differs from the <a href="#nominate">nominate subspecies</a> in the very dark, dense barring on its underside and its black ear coverts.</li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus furuitii</b></i>, described by Momiyama in 1927, is found on the <a href="/wiki/Izu_Islands" title="Izu Islands">Izu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ogasawara_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Ogasawara Islands">Ogasawara Islands</a> south of <a href="/wiki/Honsh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Honshū">Honshū</a>, Japan. It is non-migratory. It is very rare, and may only remain on a single island.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is a dark form, resembling <i>pealei</i> in colour, but darker, especially on the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus japonensis</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Gmelin" title="Johann Friedrich Gmelin">Gmelin</a> in 1788, includes <i>kleinschmidti</i>, <i>pleskei</i>, and <i>harterti,</i> and seems to refer to intergrades with <i><a href="#calidus">calidus</a></i>. It is found from northeast <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> (though it is possibly replaced by <i>pealei</i> on the coast there) and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Northern populations are migratory, while those of Japan are resident. It is similar to <i>peregrinus</i>, but the young are even darker than those of <i><a href="#anatum">anatum</a></i>.</li></ul>
<ul><li><i><b>Falco peregrinus macropus</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/William_John_Swainson" title="William John Swainson">Swainson</a> in 1837, is the Australian peregrine falcon. It is found in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in all regions except the southwest. It is non-migratory. It is similar to <i>brookei</i> in appearance, but is slightly smaller and the ear region is entirely black. The feet are proportionally large.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus madens</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Dillon_Ripley" title="Sidney Dillon Ripley">Ripley</a> and Watson in 1963, is unusual in having some <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dichromatism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual dichromatism">sexual dichromatism</a>. If the Barbary falcon (see below) is considered a distinct species, it is sometimes placed therein. It is found in the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Verde Islands">Cape Verde Islands</a>, and is non-migratory;<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> it is endangered with only six to eight pairs surviving.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Males have a rufous wash on crown, nape, ears, and back; underside conspicuously washed pinkish-brown. Females are tinged rich brown overall, especially on the crown and nape.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg/220px-FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg/330px-FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg/440px-FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="909" data-file-height="1438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FalcoMinorKeulemans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>F. p. minor</i>, illustration by <a href="/wiki/John_Gerrard_Keulemans" title="John Gerrard Keulemans">Keulemans</a>, 1874</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i><b>Falco peregrinus minor</b></i>, first described by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucien_Bonaparte" title="Charles Lucien Bonaparte">Bonaparte</a> in 1850. It was formerly often known as <i>perconfusus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie1961_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie1961-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It is sparsely and patchily distributed throughout much of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> and widespread in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>. It apparently reaches north along the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> coast as far as <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>. It is non-migratory and dark coloured. This is the smallest subspecies of peregrine, with smaller males weighing as little as approximately 300&#160;g (11&#160;oz).</li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus nesiotes</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Mayr</a> in 1941,<sup id="cite_ref-Mayr1941_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr1941-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> is found in <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> and probably also <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>. It is non-migratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Peters1979_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters1979-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><i><b>Falco peregrinus pealei</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ridgway" title="Robert Ridgway">Ridgway</a> in 1873, is also known as <a href="/wiki/Peale%27s_falcon" title="Peale&#39;s falcon">Peale's falcon</a>, and includes <i>rudolfi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-AOUp165_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOUp165-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> It is found in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> of North America, northwards from the <a href="/wiki/Puget_Sound" title="Puget Sound">Puget Sound</a> along the <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> coast (including <a href="/wiki/Haida_Gwaii" title="Haida Gwaii">Haida Gwaii</a>), along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Alaska" title="Gulf of Alaska">Gulf of Alaska</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a> to the far eastern <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">Bering Sea</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-AOUp165_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOUp165-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and may also occur on the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a> and the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a>. It is non-migratory. It is the largest subspecies, and it looks like an oversized and darker <i>tundrius</i> or like a strongly barred and large <i><a href="#anatum">anatum</a></i>. The bill is very wide.<sup id="cite_ref-Proctor_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles occasionally have pale crowns. Males weigh 700&#160;to 1,000&#160;g (1.5–2.2&#160;lb), while females weigh 1,000&#160;to 1,500&#160;g (2.2–3.3&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Whiteetal2002_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whiteetal2002-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b><a href="/wiki/Falco_peregrinus_pelegrinoides" class="mw-redirect" title="Falco peregrinus pelegrinoides">Falco peregrinus pelegrinoides</a></b></i>, first described by <a href="/wiki/Coenraad_Jacob_Temminck" title="Coenraad Jacob Temminck">Temminck</a> in 1829, is found in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> through North Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. It is most similar to <i>brookei</i>, but is markedly paler above, with a rusty neck, and is a light buff with reduced barring below. It is smaller than the <a href="#nominate">nominate subspecies</a>; females weigh around 610&#160;g (1.34&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b><a href="/wiki/Falco_peregrinus_peregrinator" class="mw-redirect" title="Falco peregrinus peregrinator">Falco peregrinus peregrinator</a></b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jakob_Sundevall" title="Carl Jakob Sundevall">Sundevall</a> in 1837, is known as the Indian peregrine falcon, black shaheen, Indian shaheen<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Shaheen_falcon" title="Shaheen falcon">shaheen falcon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wijeratne2007_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wijeratne2007-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> It was formerly sometimes known as <i>Falco atriceps</i> or <i>Falco shaheen</i>. Its range includes <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> from across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and Southeastern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. In India, the shaheen is reported from all states except <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, mainly from rocky and hilly regions. The Shaheen is also reported from the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pande2007_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande2007-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It has a clutch size of 3 to 4 eggs, with the chicks fledging time of 48 days with an average nesting success of 1.32 chicks per nest. In India, apart from nesting on cliffs, it has also been recorded as nesting on man-made structures such as buildings and cellphone transmission towers.<sup id="cite_ref-Pande2007_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande2007-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> A population estimate of 40 breeding pairs in Sri Lanka was made in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-Dottlinger1999_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dottlinger1999-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It is non-migratory, and is small and dark, with rufous underparts. In <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> this species is found to favour the higher hills while the migrant <i><a href="#calidus">calidus</a></i> is more often seen along the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Dottlinger2005_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dottlinger2005-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b><span id="nominate"></span><span id="Falco_peregrinus_peregrinus">Falco peregrinus peregrinus</span></b></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Nominate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominate">nominate</a> (first-named) subspecies, described by <a href="/wiki/Marmaduke_Tunstall" title="Marmaduke Tunstall">Tunstall</a> in 1771, breeds over much of temperate <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> in the north and the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> region and <a href="/wiki/Alpide_belt" title="Alpide belt">Alpide belt</a> in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-AOUp164_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOUp164-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> It is mainly non-migratory in Europe, but <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> in Scandinavia and Asia. Males weigh 580&#160;to 750&#160;g (1.28–1.65&#160;lb), while females weigh 925&#160;to 1,300&#160;g (2.039–2.866&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It includes <i>brevirostris</i>, <i>germanicus</i>, <i>rhenanus</i>, and <i>riphaeus</i>.</li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus radama</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Hartlaub" title="Gustav Hartlaub">Hartlaub</a> in 1861, is found in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>. It is non-migratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus submelanogenys</b></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Mathews" title="Gregory Mathews">Mathews</a> in 1912, is the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Australia_(ecoregion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest Australia (ecoregion)">Southwest Australian</a> peregrine falcon. It is found in southwest Australia and is non-migratory.</li>
<li><i><b>Falco peregrinus tundrius</b></i>, described by C.M. White in 1968, was at one time included in <i>leucogenys</i>. It is found in the Arctic tundra of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and migrates to wintering grounds in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Proctor_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Vagrant_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Vagrant birds">vagrants</a> that reach western Europe belong to this subspecies, which was previously united with <i><a href="#anatum">anatum</a></i>. It is the New World equivalent to <i><a href="#calidus">calidus</a></i>. It is smaller than <i>anatum</i>. It is also paler than <i>anatum</i>; most have a conspicuous white forehead and white in ear region, but the crown and "moustache" are very dark, unlike in <i>calidus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Proctor_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proctor-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles are browner, and less grey, than in <i>calidus</i>, and paler, sometimes almost sandy, than in <i>anatum</i>. Males weigh 500&#160;to 700&#160;g (1.1–1.5&#160;lb), while females weigh 800&#160;to 1,100&#160;g (1.8–2.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Whiteetal2002_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whiteetal2002-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Despite its current recognition as a valid subspecies, a population genetic study of both pre-decline (i.e., museum) and recovered contemporary populations failed to distinguish genetically the <i>anatum</i> and <i>tundrius</i> subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Brownetal2007_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brownetal2007-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Barbary_falcon">Barbary falcon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Barbary falcon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Barbary_falcon" title="Barbary falcon">Barbary falcon</a></div>
<p>These birds inhabit <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> regions from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> along the rim of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> to <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>.
</p><p>Barbary falcons have a red neck patch but otherwise differ in appearance from the peregrine proper merely according to <a href="/wiki/Gloger%27s_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Gloger&#39;s Rule">Gloger's Rule</a>, relating <a href="/wiki/Pigmentation" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigmentation">pigmentation</a> to <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">environmental humidity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dottlinger2002_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dottlinger2002-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The Barbary falcon has a peculiar way of flying, beating only the outer part of its wings like <a href="/wiki/Fulmar" title="Fulmar">fulmars</a> sometimes do; this also occurs in the peregrine, but less often and far less pronounced.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Barbary falcon's <a href="/wiki/Shoulder" title="Shoulder">shoulder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a> bones are stout by comparison with the peregrine, and its feet are smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie1961_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie1961-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Barbary falcons breed at different times of year than neighboring peregrine falcon subspecies,<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helbig1994_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig1994-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink1998_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink1998-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink2000_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2000-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie1961_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie1961-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WinkSauer2000_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WinkSauer2000-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink2004_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2004-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> but they are capable of interbreeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Blondel1999_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blondel1999-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> There is a 0.6–0.7% genetic distance in the peregrine-Barbary falcon ("peregrinoid") complex.<sup id="cite_ref-Wink2000_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink2000-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_behaviour">Ecology and behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ecology and behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineTubercle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PeregrineTubercle.jpg/220px-PeregrineTubercle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PeregrineTubercle.jpg/330px-PeregrineTubercle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/PeregrineTubercle.jpg/440px-PeregrineTubercle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1935" data-file-height="1545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineTubercle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Closeup of head showing nostril tubercle</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg/220px-PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg/330px-PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg/440px-PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="376" data-file-height="231" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PeregrineFalconSilhouettes.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silhouette in normal flight (left) and at the start of a stoop (right)</div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon lives mostly along <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain ranges</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="River valley">river valleys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coastline" class="mw-redirect" title="Coastline">coastlines</a>, and increasingly in <a href="/wiki/Cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Cities">cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees2001_13-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees2001-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In mild-winter regions, it is usually a permanent resident, and some individuals, especially adult males, will remain on the breeding territory. Only populations that breed in Arctic <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a> typically migrate great distances during the northern winter.<sup id="cite_ref-DOI95_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOI95-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine falcon reaches faster speeds than any other animal on the planet when performing the stoop,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> which involves soaring to a great height and then diving steeply at speeds of over 320&#160;km/h (200&#160;mph), hitting one wing of its prey so as not to harm itself on impact.<sup id="cite_ref-USFWS_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USFWS-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The air pressure from such a dive could possibly damage a bird's <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, but small bony tubercles on a falcon's nostrils are theorized to guide the powerful airflow away from the nostrils, enabling the bird to breathe more easily while diving by reducing the change in air pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-Wisconsin_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wisconsin-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> To protect their eyes, the falcons use their <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">nictitating membranes</a> (third eyelids) to spread tears and clear debris from their eyes while maintaining vision. A study testing the flight physics of an "ideal falcon" found a theoretical speed limit at 400&#160;km/h (250&#160;mph) for low-altitude flight and 625&#160;km/h (388&#160;mph) for high-altitude flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker1998_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker1998-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, Ken Franklin recorded a falcon stooping at a top speed of 389&#160;km/h (242&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-Harpole2005_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harpole2005-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The life span of peregrine falcons in the wild is up to 19 years 9 months.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Mortality in the first year is 59–70%, declining to 25–32% annually in adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Apart from such <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> threats as <a href="/wiki/Bird_collision_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Bird collision (disambiguation)">collision</a> with human-made objects, the peregrine may be killed by larger <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Michigan_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michigan-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine falcon is <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> to a range of <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a> for <a href="/wiki/Avipoxvirus" title="Avipoxvirus">Avipoxvirus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_disease_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcastle disease virus">Newcastle disease virus</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Falconid_herpesvirus_1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Falconid herpesvirus 1 (page does not exist)">Falconid herpesvirus 1</a> (and possibly other <a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">Herpesviridae</a>), and some <a href="/wiki/Mycoses" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycoses">mycoses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterial infection">bacterial infections</a>. <a href="/wiki/Endoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Endoparasite">Endoparasites</a> include <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_relictum" title="Plasmodium relictum">Plasmodium relictum</a></i> (usually not causing <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> in the peregrine falcon), <a href="/wiki/Strigeidae" title="Strigeidae">Strigeidae</a> <a href="/wiki/Trematode" class="mw-redirect" title="Trematode">trematodes</a>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Serratospiculum_amaculata&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Serratospiculum amaculata (page does not exist)">Serratospiculum amaculata</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Tapeworm" class="mw-redirect" title="Tapeworm">tapeworms</a>. Known peregrine falcon <a href="/wiki/Ectoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ectoparasite">ectoparasites</a> are <a href="/wiki/Chewing_lice" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewing lice">chewing lice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_garei" title="Ceratophyllus garei">Ceratophyllus garei</a></i> (a <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">flea</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Hippoboscidae" title="Hippoboscidae">Hippoboscidae</a> flies (<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Icosta_nigra&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Icosta nigra (page does not exist)">Icosta nigra</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ornithoctona_erythrocephala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ornithoctona erythrocephala (page does not exist)">Ornithoctona erythrocephala</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Potter2002_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potter2002-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dalgleish2003_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalgleish2003-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Raidal1999_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raidal1999-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Raidal2000_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raidal2000-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Arctic Peregrine falcons chasing away small rodent predators from their nesting territory and <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">Rough-legged Hawks</a> (<i>Buteo lagopus</i>) could use these hot spots as a nesting territory.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg/220px-Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg/330px-Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg/440px-Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2361" data-file-height="1599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_on_ship_USFWS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An immature peregrine falcon eating its prey on the deck of a ship</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg/220px-Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg/330px-Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg/440px-Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_with_common_teal_kill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult peregrine f.p. calidus consuming a common teal (duck species) in little rann of kutch, Gujarat, India</div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon feeds almost exclusively on medium-sized birds such as <a href="/wiki/Columbidae" title="Columbidae">pigeons and doves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">songbirds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wader" title="Wader">waders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckstead2001_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckstead2001-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> This falcon tends to nest on tall buildings or bridges, and these urban dwelling birds subsist mostly on different pigeons.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Worldwide, it is estimated that between 1,500 and 2,000 bird species (up to roughly a fifth of the world's bird species) are predated somewhere by these falcons. In North America, prey has varied in size from 3&#160;g (0.11&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Selasphorus" title="Selasphorus">Selasphorus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Archilochus_(bird)" title="Archilochus (bird)">Archilochus</a></i> ssp.) to a 3.1&#160;kg (6.8&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Sandhill_crane" title="Sandhill crane">sandhill crane</a> (killed in Alaska by a peregrine in a stoop), although most prey taken by peregrines weigh from 20&#160;g (0.71&#160;oz) (small <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a>) to 1,100&#160;g (2.4&#160;lb) (such as <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-bna_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bna-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown1986_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1986-66">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine falcon takes the most diverse range of bird species of any raptor in North America, with more than 300 species having fallen victim to the falcon, including nearly 100 <a href="/wiki/Shorebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Shorebird">shorebirds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod1978_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod1978-67">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Smaller <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> are regularly predated, mainly smaller falcons such as the <a href="/wiki/American_kestrel" title="American kestrel">American kestrel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharp-shinned_hawk" title="Sharp-shinned hawk">sharp-shinned hawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogan2010_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogan2010-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klem1985_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klem1985-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In urban areas, the main component of the peregrine's diet is the <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock</a> or <a href="/wiki/Feral_pigeon" title="Feral pigeon">feral pigeon</a>, which comprise 80% or more of the dietary intake for peregrines in some cities. Other common city birds are also taken regularly, including <a href="/wiki/Mourning_dove" title="Mourning dove">mourning doves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_swift" title="Common swift">common swifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_flicker" title="Northern flicker">northern flickers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">common starlings</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_robin" title="American robin">American robins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_blackbird" title="Common blackbird">common blackbirds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Magpie" title="Magpie">magpies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion</a>, <a href="/wiki/House_crow" title="House crow">house</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_crow" title="American crow">American crows</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Drewitt2008_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drewitt2008-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Other than bats taken at night,<sup id="cite_ref-Drewitt2008_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drewitt2008-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> the peregrine rarely hunts mammals, but will on occasion take small species such as <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a>. Coastal populations of the large subspecies <i>pealei</i> feed almost exclusively on <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Terres1991_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres1991-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> swamp of <a href="/wiki/Cubat%C3%A3o" title="Cubatão">Cubatão</a>, a wintering falcon of the subspecies <i>tundrius</i> was observed while successfully hunting a juvenile <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_ibis" title="Scarlet ibis">scarlet ibis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Olmos2003_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olmos2003-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Insects and reptiles make up a small proportion of the diet, which varies greatly depending on what prey is available.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckstead2001_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckstead2001-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine falcon hunts most often at dawn and dusk, when prey are most active, but also nocturnally in cities, particularly during migration periods when hunting at night may become prevalent. Nocturnal migrants taken by peregrines include species as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_cuckoo" title="Yellow-billed cuckoo">yellow-billed cuckoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black-necked_grebe" title="Black-necked grebe">black-necked grebe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia_rail" title="Virginia rail">virginia rail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Common_quail" title="Common quail">common quail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Drewitt2008_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drewitt2008-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine requires open space in order to hunt, and therefore often hunts over open water, <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a>, fields, and <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, searching for prey either from a high perch or from the air.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrlich1992_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrlich1992-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Large congregations of migrants, especially species that gather in the open like shorebirds, can be quite attractive to hunting peregrines. Once prey is spotted, it begins its stoop, folding back the tail and wings, with feet tucked.<sup id="cite_ref-Terres1991_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres1991-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Prey is typically struck and captured in mid-air; the peregrine falcon strikes its prey with a clenched foot, stunning or killing it with the impact, then turns to catch it in mid-air.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrlich1992_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrlich1992-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> If its prey is too heavy to carry, a peregrine will drop it to the ground and eat it there. If they miss the initial strike, peregrines will chase their prey in a twisting flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Treleaven1980_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treleaven1980-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Although previously thought rare, several cases of peregrines contour-hunting, i.e. using natural contours to surprise and ambush prey on the ground, have been reported and even rare cases of prey being pursued on foot. In addition, peregrines have been documented preying on chicks in nests, from birds such as kittiwakes.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins2014_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins2014-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Prey is plucked before consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Wisconsin_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wisconsin-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A recent study showed the presence of peregrines benefits non-preferred species while at the same time causing a decline in its preferred prey.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> As of 2018, the fastest recorded falcon was at 242&#160;mph (nearly 390&#160;km/h). Researchers at the University of Groningen in the Netherlands and at Oxford University used 3D computer simulations in 2018 to show that the high speed allows peregrines to gain better maneuverability and precision in strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-FALCON_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FALCON-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg/220px-Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg/330px-Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg/440px-Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1136" data-file-height="852" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Faucon_pelerin_7_mai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>At nest, France</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG/220px-Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG/330px-Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG/440px-Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2299" data-file-height="2360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Falco_peregrinus_MWNH_0671.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon is sexually mature at one to three years of age, but in larger populations they breed after two to three years of age. A pair <a href="/wiki/Mates_for_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Mates for life">mates for life</a> and returns to the same nesting spot annually. The courtship flight includes a mix of aerial acrobatics, precise spirals, and steep dives.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter2002_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potter2002-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The male passes prey it has caught to the female in mid-air. To make this possible, the female actually flies upside-down to receive the food from the male's talons.
</p><p>During the breeding season, the peregrine falcon is territorial; nesting pairs are usually more than 1&#160;km (0.62&#160;mi) apart, and often much farther, even in areas with large numbers of pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood2001_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood2001-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The distance between nests ensures sufficient food supply for pairs and their chicks. Within a breeding territory, a pair may have several nesting ledges; the number used by a pair can vary from one or two up to seven in a 16-year period.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg/220px-Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg/330px-Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg/440px-Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2160" data-file-height="1428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_falcon_chick_banding_Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peregrine chicks in a nest on the <a href="/wiki/Verrazzano-Narrows_Bridge" title="Verrazzano-Narrows Bridge">Verrazzano-Narrows Bridge</a> in New York City being <a href="/wiki/Bird_banding" class="mw-redirect" title="Bird banding">banded</a></div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon nests in a scrape, normally on cliff edges.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The female chooses a nest site, where she scrapes a shallow hollow in the loose soil, sand, gravel, or dead vegetation in which to lay eggs. No nest materials are added.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter2002_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potter2002-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Cliff nests are generally located under an overhang, on ledges with vegetation. South-facing sites are favoured.<sup id="cite_ref-Terres1991_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres1991-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In some regions, as in parts of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and on the west coast of northern North America, large tree hollows are used for nesting. Before the demise of most European peregrines, a large population of peregrines in central and western Europe used the disused nests of other large birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckstead2001_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckstead2001-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In remote, undisturbed areas such as the Arctic, steep slopes and even low rocks and mounds may be used as nest sites. In many parts of its range, peregrines now also nest regularly on tall buildings or bridges; these human-made structures used for breeding closely resemble the natural cliff ledges that the peregrine prefers for its nesting locations.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blood2001_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood2001-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pair defends the chosen nest site against other peregrines, and often against <a href="/wiki/Raven" title="Raven">ravens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, and if ground-nesting, also such mammals as <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolverine" title="Wolverine">wolverines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felid" class="mw-redirect" title="Felid">felids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">wolves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mountain_lion" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountain lion">mountain lions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood2001_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood2001-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Both nests and (less frequently) adults are predated by larger-bodied raptorial birds like <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">owls</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcons</a>. The most serious predators of peregrine nests in North America and Europe are the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian eagle owl">Eurasian eagle owl</a>. When reintroductions have been attempted for peregrines, the most serious impediments were these two species of owls routinely picking off nestlings, fledglings and adults by night.<sup id="cite_ref-Walton1988_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walton1988-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brambilla2006_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brambilla2006-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Peregrines defending their nests have managed to kill raptors as large as <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a> (both of which they normally avoid as potential predators) that have come too close to the nest by ambushing them in a full stoop.<sup id="cite_ref-bna2_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bna2-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In one instance, when a <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> killed a newly fledged peregrine, the larger owl was in turn killed by a stooping peregrine parent.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous1988_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous1988-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The date of egg-laying varies according to locality, but is generally from February to March in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, and from July to August in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, although the Australian subspecies <i>macropus</i> may breed as late as November, and <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial</a> populations may nest anytime between June and December. If the eggs are lost early in the nesting season, the female usually lays another clutch, although this is extremely rare in the Arctic due to the short summer season.
