Generally three to four eggs, but sometimes as few as one or as many as five, are laid in the scrape.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are white to buff with red or brown markings.<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> They are incubated for 29 to 33 days, mainly by the female,<sup id="cite_ref-Terres1991_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres1991-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> with the male also helping with the incubation of the eggs during the day, but only the female incubating them at night. The average number of young found in nests is 2.5, and the average number that fledge is about 1.5, due to the occasional production of infertile eggs and various natural losses of nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-White94_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White94-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wisconsin_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wisconsin-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michigan_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michigan-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After hatching, the chicks (called "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eyas" class="extiw" title="wikt:eyas">eyases</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-raptorresource_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raptorresource-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>) are covered with creamy-white down and have disproportionately large feet.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood2001_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood2001-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The male (called the "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tiercel" class="extiw" title="wikt:tiercel">tiercel</a>") and the female (simply called the "falcon") both leave the nest to gather prey to feed the young.<sup id="cite_ref-Wisconsin_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wisconsin-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The hunting territory of the parents can extend a radius of 19 to 24&#160;km (12 to 15&#160;mi) from the nest site.<sup id="cite_ref-Towry1987_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Towry1987-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Chicks <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> 42 to 46 days after hatching, and remain dependent on their parents for up to two months.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_falconry">Use in falconry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Use in falconry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">Falconry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Falconry_sport_of_kings_(1920)_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png/220px-Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png/330px-Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png/440px-Falconry_sport_of_kings_%281920%29_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png 2x" data-file-width="495" data-file-height="653" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Falconry_sport_of_kings_(1920)_Peregrine_falcon_striking_red_grouse.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tame peregrine striking a <a href="/wiki/Red_grouse" title="Red grouse">red grouse</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Agassiz_Fuertes" title="Louis Agassiz Fuertes">Louis Agassiz Fuertes</a> (1920)</div></div></div>
<p>The peregrine falcon is a highly admired falconry bird, and has been used in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> for more than 3,000 years, beginning with nomads in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Blood2001_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blood2001-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Its advantages in falconry include not only its athleticism and eagerness to hunt, but an equable disposition that leads to it being one of the easier falcons to train.<sup id="cite_ref-Beebe1984_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beebe1984-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine falcon has the additional advantage of a natural flight style of circling above the falconer ("waiting on") for game to be flushed, and then performing an effective and exciting high-speed diving stoop to take the <a href="/wiki/Quarry_(prey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quarry (prey)">quarry</a>. The speed of the stoop not only allows the falcon to catch fast flying birds, it also enhances the falcon's ability to exert maneuvers to catch highly agile prey,<sup id="cite_ref-Mills2018_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills2018-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and allows the falcon to deliver a knock out blow with a fist-like clenched talon against game that may be much larger than itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Scholz_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scholz-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Additionally the versatility of the species, with agility allowing capture of smaller birds and a strength and attacking style allowing capture of game much larger than themselves, combined with the wide size range of the many peregrine subspecies, means there is a subspecies suitable to almost any size and type of game bird. This size range, evolved to fit various environments and prey species, is from the larger females of the largest subspecies to the smaller males of the smallest subspecies, approximately five to one (approximately 1500&#160;g to 300&#160;g). The males of smaller and medium-sized subspecies, and the females of the smaller subspecies, excel in the taking of swift and agile small game birds such as dove, quail, and smaller ducks. The females of the larger subspecies are capable of taking large and powerful game birds such as the largest of duck species, pheasant, and grouse.
</p><p>Peregrine falcons handled by falconers are also occasionally used to scare away birds at airports to reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">bird-plane strikes</a>, improving air-traffic safety.<sup id="cite_ref-Kuzir1999_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzir1999-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> They were also used to intercept homing pigeons during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Enderson2005_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enderson2005-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peregrine falcons have been successfully bred in captivity, both for falconry and for release back into the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-SCPBRG_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCPBRG-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Until 2004 nearly all peregrines used for falconry in the US were captive-bred from the progeny of falcons taken before the US <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered Species Act">Endangered Species Act</a> was enacted and from those few infusions of wild genes available from Canada and special circumstances. Peregrine falcons were removed from the United States' endangered species list in 1999. The successful recovery program was aided by the effort and knowledge of falconers – in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/The_Peregrine_Fund" title="The Peregrine Fund">The Peregrine Fund</a> and state and federal agencies – through a technique called <a href="/wiki/Hack_(falconry)" title="Hack (falconry)">hacking</a>. Finally, after years of close work with the US Fish and Wildlife Service, a limited take of wild peregrines was allowed in 2004, the first wild peregrines taken specifically for falconry in over 30 years.
</p><p>The development of captive breeding methods has led to peregrines being commercially available for falconry use, thus mostly eliminating the need to capture wild birds for support of falconry. The main reason for taking wild peregrines at this point is to maintain healthy genetic diversity in the breeding lines. Hybrids of peregrines and <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcons</a> are also available that can combine the best features of both species to create what many consider to be the ultimate falconry bird for the taking of larger game such as the <a href="/wiki/Sage-grouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Sage-grouse">sage-grouse</a>. These hybrids combine the greater size, strength, and horizontal speed of the gyrfalcon with the natural propensity to stoop and greater warm weather tolerance of the peregrine.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_due_to_pesticides">Decline due to pesticides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Decline due to pesticides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The peregrine falcon became an endangered species over much of its range because of the use of <a href="/wiki/Organochlorine_pesticide" class="mw-redirect" title="Organochlorine pesticide">organochlorine pesticides</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>, during the 1950s, '60s, and '70s.<sup id="cite_ref-Cade1988_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cade1988-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Pesticide <a href="/wiki/Biomagnification" title="Biomagnification">biomagnification</a> caused <a href="/wiki/Organochlorine" class="mw-redirect" title="Organochlorine">organochlorine</a> to build up in the falcons' fat tissues, reducing the amount of calcium in their eggshells. With thinner shells, fewer falcon eggs survived until hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrlich1992_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrlich1992-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown1976_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1976-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In several parts of the world, such as the eastern <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, this species became <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpated</a> (locally extinct) as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-Snow1998_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow1998-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> An alternate point of view is that populations in the eastern North America had vanished due to hunting and egg collection.<sup id="cite_ref-Lehr2000_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lehr2000-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Following the ban of organochlorine pesticides, the reproductive success of Peregrines increased in Scotland in terms of territory occupancy and breeding success, although spatial variation in recovery rates indicate that in some areas Peregrines were also impacted by other factors such as persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_efforts">Recovery efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Recovery efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Peregrine falcon recovery teams breed the species in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-RDigest_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RDigest-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The chicks are usually fed through a chute or with a <a href="/wiki/Hand_puppet" title="Hand puppet">hand puppet</a> mimicking a peregrine's head, so they cannot see to <a href="/wiki/Imprinting_(psychology)" title="Imprinting (psychology)">imprint</a> on the human trainers.<sup id="cite_ref-DOI95_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOI95-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Then, when they are old enough, the rearing box is opened, allowing the bird to train its wings. As the fledgling gets stronger, feeding is reduced, forcing the bird to learn to hunt. This procedure is called <a href="/wiki/Hack_(falconry)" title="Hack (falconry)">hacking back to the wild</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Aitken2004_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken2004-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> To release a captive-bred falcon, the bird is placed in a special cage at the top of a tower or cliff ledge for some days or so, allowing it to acclimate itself to its future environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Aitken2004_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken2004-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Worldwide recovery efforts have been remarkably successful.<sup id="cite_ref-RDigest_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RDigest-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The widespread restriction of DDT use eventually allowed released birds to breed successfully.<sup id="cite_ref-DOI95_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOI95-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine falcon was removed from the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a> <a href="/wiki/Endangered_Species" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered Species">Endangered Species</a> list on 25 August 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-DOI95_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOI95-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Henny1981_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henny1981-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some controversy has existed over the origins of captive breeding stock used by <a href="/wiki/The_Peregrine_Fund" title="The Peregrine Fund">The Peregrine Fund</a> in the recovery of peregrine falcons throughout the contiguous United States. Several peregrine subspecies were included in the breeding stock, including birds of Eurasian origin. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpation</a> of the eastern <i>anatum</i> (<i>Falco peregrinus anatum</i>), the near extirpation of the <i>anatum</i> in the Midwest, and the limited gene pool within North American breeding stock, the inclusion of non-native <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> was justified to optimize the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> found within the species as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-Cade2003_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cade2003-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1970s, peregrine falcons in Finland experienced a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a> as a result of large declines associated with <a href="/wiki/Bio-accumulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bio-accumulation">bio-accumulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochloride</a> pesticides. However, the genetic diversity of peregrines in Finland is similar to other populations, indicating that high dispersal rates have maintained the genetic diversity of this species.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since Peregrine eggs and chicks are still often targeted by illegal poachers,<sup id="cite_ref-scientific-web_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scientific-web-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> it is common practice not to publicize unprotected nest locations.<sup id="cite_ref-ABA2005_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABA2005-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs,_Dover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg/220px-Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg/330px-Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg/440px-Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs%2C_Dover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peregrine_off_White_Cliffs,_Dover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peregrine flying along the coastline of the <a href="/wiki/White_Cliffs_of_Dover" title="White Cliffs of Dover">White Cliffs of Dover</a> in England</div></div></div>
<p>Populations of the peregrine falcon have bounced back in most parts of the world. In the United Kingdom, there has been a recovery of populations since the crash of the 1960s. This has been greatly assisted by conservation and protection work led by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a>. The RSPB has estimated that there are 1,402 breeding pairs in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcnews-11may2011_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews-11may2011-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rspb_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In Canada, where peregrines were identified as endangered in 1978 (in the <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a> territory of northern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> that year, only a single breeding pair was identified<sup id="cite_ref-COSEWIC-Yahoo_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COSEWIC-Yahoo-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>), the <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_the_Status_of_Endangered_Wildlife_in_Canada" title="Committee on the Status of Endangered Wildlife in Canada">Committee on the Status of Endangered Wildlife in Canada</a> declared the species no longer at risk in December 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-COSEWIC_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COSEWIC-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Peregrines now breed in many mountainous and coastal areas, especially in the west and north, and nest in some urban areas, capitalising on the urban <a href="/wiki/Feral_pigeon" title="Feral pigeon">feral pigeon</a> populations for food.<sup id="cite_ref-rspb2003_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rspb2003-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>, a nest prevented restoration of mobile telephony services for several months, after <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a> engineers despatched to repair a faulty <a href="/wiki/Transmitter_mast" class="mw-redirect" title="Transmitter mast">transmitter mast</a> discovered a nest in the mast, and were prevented by the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act">Wildlife and Countryside Act</a> – on pain of a possible prison sentence – from proceeding with repairs until the chicks fledged.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcnews-15apr2013_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews-15apr2013-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In many parts of the world peregrine falcons have adapted to urban habitats, nesting on <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedrals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscraper</a> window ledges, tower blocks,<sup id="cite_ref-bbcnews-3jun2005_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews-3jun2005-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and the towers of <a href="/wiki/Suspension_bridge" title="Suspension bridge">suspension bridges</a>. Many of these nesting birds are encouraged, sometimes gathering media attention and often monitored by cameras.<sup id="cite_ref-Navarro2009_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro2009-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cultural significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png/170px-2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png/255px-2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png/340px-2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png 2x" data-file-width="1080" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2007_ID_Proof_Rev.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> <a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" class="mw-redirect" title="50 State Quarters">state quarter</a></div></div></div>
<p>Due to its striking hunting technique, the peregrine has often been associated with aggression and martial prowess. The Ancient Egyptian solar deity Ra was often represented as a man with the head of a Peregrine Falcon adorned with the solar disk. <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> (c. 800–1500) used the peregrine, along with several other birds of prey, in imagery as a symbol of "aerial (celestial) power" and buried men of high status in costumes associating to the ferocity of raptorial birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Krech2009_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krech2009-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">late Middle Ages</a>, the Western European nobility that used peregrines for hunting, considered the bird associated with <a href="/wiki/Prince" title="Prince">princes</a> in formal hierarchies of birds of prey, just below the <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcon</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/King" title="King">kings</a>. It was considered "a royal bird, more armed by its courage than its claws". Terminology used by peregrine breeders also used the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> term <i lang="fro"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gentil#Old_French" class="extiw" title="wikt:gentil">gentil</a></i>, "of noble birth; aristocratic", particularly with the peregrine.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1970_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1970-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peregrine falcon is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_animals" title="List of national animals">national animal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>. Since 1927, the peregrine falcon has been the official mascot of <a href="/wiki/Bowling_Green_State_University" title="Bowling Green State University">Bowling Green State University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bowling_Green,_Ohio" title="Bowling Green, Ohio">Bowling Green, Ohio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bowlinggreen_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowlinggreen-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The 2007 U.S. <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> <a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" class="mw-redirect" title="50 State Quarters">state quarter</a> features a peregrine falcon.<sup id="cite_ref-Shalaway2007_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shalaway2007-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The peregrine falcon has been designated the <a href="/wiki/List_of_official_city_birds" title="List of official city birds">official city bird</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UCC_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCC-115">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The Peregrine</i>, by <a href="/wiki/J._A._Baker" title="J. A. Baker">J. A. Baker</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> is widely regarded as one of the best nature books in English written in the twentieth century. Admirers of the book include <a href="/wiki/Robert_Macfarlane_(writer)" title="Robert Macfarlane (writer)">Robert Macfarlane</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Cocker" title="Mark Cocker">Mark Cocker</a>, who regards the book as "one of the most outstanding books on nature in the twentieth century"<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_Herzog" title="Werner Herzog">Werner Herzog</a>, who called it "the one book I would ask you to read if you want to make films",<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and said elsewhere "it has prose of the calibre that we have not seen since <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">Joseph Conrad</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the book, Baker recounts, in diary form, his detailed observations of peregrines (and their interaction with other birds) near his home in <a href="/wiki/Chelmsford" title="Chelmsford">Chelmsford</a>, Essex, over a single winter from October to April.
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Perlin_(falconry)" title="Perlin (falconry)">Perlin</a>, a hybrid of the peregrine falcon and the <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a> (<i>Falco columbarius</i>)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Explanatory_notes">Explanatory notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peregrine_falcon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Explanatory notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Contra</i> <a href="#CITEREFHelbigSeiboldBednarekBrüning1994">Helbig et al. 1994</a>, <a href="#CITEREFWinkSeiboldLotfikhahBednarek1998">Wink et al. 1998</a>. The supposed <a href="/wiki/Basal_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Basal (biology)">basal</a> position of the hierofalcons was due to them having a <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome <i>b</i></a> <a href="/wiki/Numt" class="mw-redirect" title="Numt">numt</a>: see <a href="#CITEREFWinkSauer-Gürth2000">Wink &amp; Sauer-Gürth 2000</a></span>
